	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Uralic languages</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Urali_language" title="Urali language">Urali language</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width:22em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: lime;">Uralic</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: lime;">Uralian</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Geographic<br />distribution</th><td><a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Asia" title="North Asia">North Asia</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Linguistic classification</a></th><td>One of the world's primary <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language families</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Proto-language</th><td><a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_language" title="Proto-Uralic language">Proto-Uralic</a></td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row">Subdivisions</th><td>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ugric_languages" title="Ugric languages">Ugric</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mordvinic_languages" title="Mordvinic languages">Mordvinic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Permic_languages" title="Permic languages">Permic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic</a></li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-5" title="ISO 639-5">ISO 639-5</a></th><td><samp><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/urj" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:urj">urj</a></samp></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td><samp><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/ural1272">ural1272</a></samp><sup id="cite_ref-Glottolog_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glottolog-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_(en).png" class="image"><img alt="Linguistic map of the Uralic languages (en).png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png/300px-Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="225" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png/450px-Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png/600px-Linguistic_map_of_the_Uralic_languages_%28en%29.png 2x" data-file-width="2065" data-file-height="1548" /></a><div style="text-align:left;">Geographical distribution of the Uralic languages</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Finno_ugric_languages.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Finno_ugric_languages.png/220px-Finno_ugric_languages.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Finno_ugric_languages.png/330px-Finno_ugric_languages.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Finno_ugric_languages.png/440px-Finno_ugric_languages.png 2x" data-file-width="1042" data-file-height="827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Finno_ugric_languages.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uralic languages (Meänkieli, Kven and Ludic are debated)</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Uralic languages</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;yes&#39;">j</span><span title="/ʊəˈr/: &#39;ur&#39; in &#39;plurality&#39;">ʊəˈr</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; sometimes called <b>Uralian languages</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;yes&#39;">j</span><span title="/ʊəˈr/: &#39;ur&#39; in &#39;plurality&#39;">ʊəˈr</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>) form a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a> of 38<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">languages</a> spoken by approximately 25<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million people, predominantly in Northern <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and in eastern and southern <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>. The Uralic languages with the most native speakers are <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>, which are official languages in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, respectively. Other Uralic languages with significant numbers of speakers are <a href="/wiki/Erzya_language" title="Erzya language">Erzya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moksha_language" title="Moksha language">Moksha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">Udmurt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Russia" title="Languages of Russia">officially recognized languages</a> in various regions of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.
</p><p>The name "Uralic" derives from the fact that the areas where the languages are spoken are found on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric</a> is sometimes used as a synonym for Uralic, though Finno-Ugric is widely understood to exclude the <a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Scholars who do not accept the traditional notion that Samoyedic split first from the rest of the Uralic family may treat the terms as synonymous.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Uralic">Proto-Uralic</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homeland">Homeland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Homeland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_homeland_hypotheses" title="Proto-Uralic homeland hypotheses">Proto-Uralic homeland hypotheses</a></div>
<p>Proposed homelands of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_language" title="Proto-Uralic language">Proto-Uralic language</a> include:
</p>
<ul><li>The vicinity of the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga River</a>, west of the Urals, close to the Urheimat of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, or to the east and southeast of the Urals. Historian <a href="/wiki/Gyula_L%C3%A1szl%C3%B3" title="Gyula László">Gyula László</a> places its origin in the forest zone between the <a href="/wiki/Oka_River" title="Oka River">Oka River</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>. E.&#160;N. Setälä and M.&#160;Zsirai place it between the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kama_River" title="Kama River">Kama Rivers</a>. According to E.&#160;Itkonen, the ancestral area extended to the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. Jaakko Häkkinen identifies Proto-Uralic with Eneolithic Garino-Bor (Turbin) culture 3,000-2,500 YBP located in the Lower Kama Basin.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>P.&#160;Hajdu has suggested a homeland in western and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Juha_Janhunen" title="Juha Janhunen">Juha Janhunen</a> suggests a homeland in between the <a href="/wiki/Ob_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Ob river">Ob</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yenisei" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei">Yenisei</a> drainage areas in <a href="/wiki/Central_Siberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Siberia">Central Siberia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Janhunen2009_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janhunen2009-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_evidence">Genetic evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Genetic evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The characteristic <a href="/wiki/Genetic_marker" title="Genetic marker">genetic marker</a> of peoples speaking Uralic languages is <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_N-M231" title="Haplogroup N-M231">haplogroup N1c</a>-Tat (Y-DNA). Samoyedic peoples mainly have more N1b-P43 than N1c.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Haplogroup N originated in the northern part of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in 20,000–25,000&#160;years BP<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and spread to north <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>. Subgroup N1c1 is frequently seen in non-Samoyedic peoples, N1c2 in <a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_peoples" title="Samoyedic peoples">Samoyedic peoples</a>. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_Z" title="Haplogroup Z">haplogroup Z (mtDNA)</a>, found with low frequency in <a href="/wiki/Saami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Saami people">Saami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">Finns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberians</a>, is related to the migration of people speaking Uralic languages.
</p><p>In 2019, a study based on genetics, archaeology and linguistics, found that Uralic speakers arrived from the East, specifically from Siberia, into the Baltic region at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> some 2,500&#160;years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_attestations">Early attestations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early attestations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first plausible mention of a people speaking a Uralic language is in <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Germania_(book)" title="Germania (book)">Germania</a></i> (c. 98 AD),<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> mentioning the <i><a href="/wiki/Fenni" title="Fenni">Fenni</a></i> (usually interpreted as referring to the <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami people">Sami</a>) and two other possibly Uralic tribes living in the farthest reaches of Scandinavia. There are many possible earlier mentions, including the <a href="/wiki/Iyrcae" title="Iyrcae">Iyrcae</a> (perhaps related to Yugra) described by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> living in what is now European Russia, and the <a href="/wiki/Budini" title="Budini">Budini</a>, described by Herodotus as notably red-haired (a characteristic feature of the <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_people" title="Udmurt people">Udmurts</a>) and living in northeast Ukraine and/or adjacent parts of Russia. In the late 15th century, European scholars noted the resemblance of the names <i>Hungaria</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Yugra" title="Yugra">Yugria</a></i>, the names of settlements east of the Ural. They assumed a connection but did not seek linguistic evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Sebeok-2002_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sebeok-2002-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uralic_studies">Uralic studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Uralic studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png/220px-Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png/330px-Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png/440px-Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="530" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herberstein-Moscovia-NE.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Siberian origin of Hungarians was long hypothesized by European scholars. Here, <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_von_Herberstein" title="Sigismund von Herberstein">Sigismund von Herberstein</a>'s 1549 map of <a href="/wiki/Moscovia" class="mw-redirect" title="Moscovia">Moscovia</a> shows "<a href="/wiki/Yugra" title="Yugra">Yugra</a> from where the <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a> originated" (<i>Iuhra inde ungaroru origo</i>), east of the <a href="/wiki/Ob_River" title="Ob River">Ob River</a>. The Ural Mountains in the middle of the maps are labeled <i>Montes dicti Cingulus Terræ</i> ("The mountains called the Girdle of the Earth")</div></div></div>
<p>The affinity of <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a> was first proposed in the late 17th century. Three candidates can be credited for the discovery: the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> scholar <a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Vogel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Martin Vogel (page does not exist)">Martin Vogel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Georg_Stiernhielm" title="Georg Stiernhielm">Georg Stiernhielm</a> and the Swedish courtier <a href="/wiki/Bengt_Skytte" title="Bengt Skytte">Bengt Skytte</a>. Vogel's unpublished study of the relationship, commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosimo III">Cosimo III</a> of Tuscany, was clearly the most modern of these: he established several <a href="/wiki/Grammatical" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical">grammatical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lexical_analysis" title="Lexical analysis">lexical</a> parallels between Finnish and Hungarian as well as <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami</a>. Stiernhelm commented on the similarities of Sami, Estonian and Finnish, and also on a few similar words between Finnish and Hungarian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198129_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198129-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988793–794_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988793–794-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> These authors were the first to outline what was to become the classification of the Finno-Ugric, and later Uralic family. This proposal received some of its initial impetus from the fact that these languages, unlike most of the other languages spoken in Europe, are not part of what is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> family. In 1717, Swedish professor <a href="/wiki/Olaus_Rudbeckius,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Olaus Rudbeckius, Jr.">Olof Rudbeck</a> proposed about 100 etymologies connecting Finnish and Hungarian, of which about 40 are still considered valid.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Several early reports comparing Finnish or Hungarian with Mordvin, Mari or Khanty were additionally collected by <a href="/wiki/Leibniz" class="mw-redirect" title="Leibniz">Leibniz</a> and edited by his assistant <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_von_Eckhart" title="Johann Georg von Eckhart">Johann Georg von Eckhart</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198129–30_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198129–30-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
In 1730, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Johan_von_Strahlenberg" title="Philip Johan von Strahlenberg">Philip Johan von Strahlenberg</a> published his book <i lang="de" title="German language text">Das Nord- und Ostliche Theil von Europa und Asia</i> (<i>The North and East Parts of Europe and Asia</i>), surveying the geography, peoples and languages of Russia. All the main groups of the Uralic languages were already identified here.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988795–796_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988795–796-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, these relationships were not widely accepted. Hungarian intellectuals especially were not interested in the theory and preferred to assume connections with <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> tribes, an attitude characterized by Merritt Ruhlen as due to "the wild unfettered <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> of the epoch".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Still, in spite of this hostile climate, the Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1nos_Sajnovics" title="János Sajnovics">János Sajnovics</a> travelled with <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_Hell" title="Maximilian Hell">Maximilian Hell</a> to survey the alleged relationship between Hungarian and Sami. Sajnovics published his results in 1770, arguing for a relationship based on several grammatical features.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988796–798_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988796–798-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 1799, the Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Gyarmathi" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Gyarmathi">Sámuel Gyarmathi</a> published the most complete work on Finno-Ugric to that date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988798_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988798-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_(1897).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/300px-Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/450px-Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg/600px-Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_%281897%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="4648" data-file-height="2744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uralic_languages_in_the_Russian_Empire_(1897).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uralic languages in the Russian Empire (Russian Census of 1897; the census was not held in Finland because it was an autonomous area)</div></div></div>
<p>Up to the beginning of the 19th century, knowledge on the Uralic languages spoken in Russia had remained restricted to scanty observations by travelers. Already Finnish historian <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Gabriel_Porthan" title="Henrik Gabriel Porthan">Henrik Gabriel Porthan</a> had stressed that further progress would require dedicated field missions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198632_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198632-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> One of the first of these was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Anders_Johan_Sj%C3%B6gren" title="Anders Johan Sjögren">Anders Johan Sjögren</a>, who brought the <a href="/wiki/Vepsians" title="Vepsians">Vepsians</a> to general knowledge and elucidated in detail the relatedness of Finnish and <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198644–46_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198644–46-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Still more extensive were the field research expeditions made in the 1840s by <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Castr%C3%A9n" title="Matthias Castrén">Matthias Castrén</a> (1813–1852) and <a href="/wiki/Antal_Reguly" title="Antal Reguly">Antal Reguly</a> (1819–1858), who focused especially on the Samoyedic and the <a href="/wiki/Ob-Ugric_languages" title="Ob-Ugric languages">Ob-Ugric languages</a>, respectively. Reguly's materials were worked on by the Hungarian linguist Pál Hunfalvy (1810–1891) and German Josef Budenz (1836–1892), who both supported the Uralic affinity of Hungarian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988801–803_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988801–803-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Budenz was the first scholar to bring this result to popular consciousness in Hungary, and to attempt a reconstruction of the Proto-Finno-Ugric grammar and lexicon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988803–804_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988803–804-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Another late-19th-century Hungarian contribution is that of Ignácz Halász (1855–1901), who published extensive comparative material of Finno-Ugric and Samoyedic in the 1890s,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and whose work is at the base of today's wide acceptance of the inclusion of Samoyedic as a part of Uralic.<sup id="cite_ref-Szabo69_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szabo69-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, in the autonomous <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a>, a chair for Finnish language and linguistics at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">University of Helsinki</a> was created in 1850, first held by Castrén.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988799–800_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988799–800-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1883, the <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugrian_Society" title="Finno-Ugrian Society">Finno-Ugrian Society</a> was founded in Helsinki on the proposal of <a href="/wiki/Otto_Donner" title="Otto Donner">Otto Donner</a>, which would lead to Helsinki overtaking St. Petersburg as the chief northern center of research of the Uralic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198649_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKorhonen198649-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> During the late 19th and early 20th century (until the separation of Finland from Russia following the <a href="/wiki/Russian_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian revolution">Russian revolution</a>), a large number of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stipendiate" class="extiw" title="wikt:stipendiate">stipendiates</a> were sent by the Society to survey the still less known Uralic languages. Major researchers of this period included Heikki Paasonen (studying especially the <a href="/wiki/Mordvinic_languages" title="Mordvinic languages">Mordvinic languages</a>), Yrjö Wichmann (studying <a href="/wiki/Permic_languages" title="Permic languages">Permic</a>), Artturi Kannisto (<a href="/wiki/Mansi_language" title="Mansi language">Mansi</a>), Kustaa Fredrik Karjalainen (<a href="/wiki/Khanty_language" title="Khanty language">Khanty</a>), Toivo Lehtisalo (<a href="/wiki/Nenets_languages" title="Nenets languages">Nenets</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Kai_Donner" title="Kai Donner">Kai Donner</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kamassian_language" title="Kamassian language">Kamass</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWickman1988810–811_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWickman1988810–811-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The vast amounts of data collected on these expeditions would provide edition work for later generations of Finnish Uralicists for more than a century.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>The Uralic family comprises nine undisputed groups with no consensus classification between them. (Some of the proposals are listed in the next section.) An agnostic approach treats them as separate branches.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Obsolete or native names are displayed in italics.
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a></b> (Fennic, Baltic Finnic, Balto-Finnic, Balto-Fennic)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a></b> (Magyar)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Khanty_language" title="Khanty language">Khanty</a></b> (<i>Ostyak, Handi, Hantõ</i>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mansi_language" title="Mansi language">Mansi</a></b> (<i>Vogul</i>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a></b> (<i>Cheremis</i>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mordvinic_languages" title="Mordvinic languages">Mordvinic</a></b> (Mordvin, Mordvinian)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Permic_languages" title="Permic languages">Permic</a></b> (Permian)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami</a></b> (Saami, Samic, Saamic, <i>Lappic, Lappish</i>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic</a></b> (Samoyed)</li></ul>
<p>There is also historical evidence of a number of extinct languages of uncertain affiliation:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Merya_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Merya language">Merya</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Muromian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Muromian language">Muromian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Meshcherian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Meshcherian language">Meshcherian</a> (until 16th century?)</li></ul>
<p>Traces of Finno-Ugric substrata, especially in toponymy, in the northern part of European Russia have been proposed as evidence for even more extinct Uralic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/File:UralicTree.svg" class="image"><img alt="UralicTree.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/UralicTree.svg/900px-UralicTree.svg.png" decoding="async" width="900" height="624" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/UralicTree.svg/1350px-UralicTree.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/UralicTree.svg/1800px-UralicTree.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1010" data-file-height="700" /></a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_classification">Traditional classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Traditional classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All Uralic languages are thought to have descended, through independent processes of <a href="/wiki/Language_change" title="Language change">language change</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic_language" title="Proto-Uralic language">Proto-Uralic</a>. The internal structure of the Uralic family has been debated since the family was first proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcantonio-p55-68_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcantonio-p55-68-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Doubts about the validity of most of the proposed higher-order branchings (grouping the nine undisputed families) are becoming more common.<sup id="cite_ref-Marcantonio-p55-68_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marcantonio-p55-68-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SalmTax_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmTax-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A traditional classification of the Uralic languages has existed since the late 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> It has enjoyed frequent adaptation in whole or in part in encyclopedias, handbooks, and overviews of the Uralic family. Donner's model is as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ugric_languages" title="Ugric languages">Ugric</a> (Ugrian)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ob-Ugric_languages" title="Ob-Ugric languages">Ob-Ugric</a> (Ob Ugrian)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Khanty_language" title="Khanty language">Khanty</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mansi_language" title="Mansi language">Mansi</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Permic_languages" title="Finno-Permic languages">Finno-Permic</a> (Permian-Finnic)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Permic_languages" title="Permic languages">Permic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Volgaic_languages" title="Finno-Volgaic languages">Finno-Volgaic</a> (Finno-Cheremisic, Finno-Mari)
<ul><li>Volga-Finnic
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mordvinic_languages" title="Mordvinic languages">Mordvinic</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Lappic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Finno-Lappic languages">Finno-Lappic</a> (Finno-Saamic, Finno-Samic)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>At Donner's time, the <a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic languages</a> were still poorly known, and he was not able to address their position. As they became better known in the early 20th century, they were found to be quite divergent, and they were assumed to have separated already early on. The terminology adopted for this was "Uralic" for the entire family, "<a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric</a>" for the non-Samoyedic languages (though "Finno-Ugric" has, to this day, remained in use also as a synonym for the whole family). <a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric</a> and Samoyedic are listed in <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-5" title="ISO 639-5">ISO 639-5</a> as primary branches of Uralic.
</p><p>Nodes of the traditional family tree recognized in some overview sources:
</p>

<p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_hajdu"><sup>a. Hajdú describes the Ugric and Volgaic groups as areal units.</sup></span> 
</p><p><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_austerlitz"><sup>b. Austerlitz accepts narrower-than-traditional Finno-Ugric and Finno-Permic groups that exclude Samic</sup></span> 
</p><p>Little explicit evidence has however been presented in favour of Donner's model since his original proposal, and numerous alternate schemes have been proposed. Especially in Finland, there has been a growing tendency to reject the Finno-Ugric intermediate protolanguage.<sup id="cite_ref-SalmTax_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmTax-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A recent competing proposal instead unites Ugric and Samoyedic in an "East Uralic" group for which shared innovations can be noted.<sup id="cite_ref-EastUralic_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EastUralic-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Finno-Permic grouping still holds some support, though the arrangement of its subgroups is a matter of some dispute. Mordvinic is commonly seen as particularly closely related to or part of Finno-Samic.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The term <i><a href="/wiki/Volga_Finns" title="Volga Finns">Volgaic</a></i> (or <i>Volga-Finnic</i>) was used to denote a branch previously believed to include Mari, Mordvinic and a number of the extinct languages, but it is now obsolete<sup id="cite_ref-SalmTax_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SalmTax-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and considered a geographic classification rather than a linguistic one.
</p><p>Within Ugric, uniting Mansi with Hungarian rather than Khanty has been a competing hypothesis to Ob-Ugric.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexical_isoglosses">Lexical isoglosses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lexical isoglosses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lexicostatistics" title="Lexicostatistics">Lexicostatistics</a> has been used in defense of the traditional family tree. A recent re-evaluation of the evidence<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> however fails to find support for Finno-Ugric and Ugric, suggesting four lexically distinct branches (Finno-Permic, Hungarian, Ob-Ugric and Samoyedic).
</p><p>One alternate proposal for a family tree, with emphasis on the development of numerals, is as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Janhunen2009_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janhunen2009-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Uralic (*kektä "2", *wixti "5" / "10")
<ul><li>Samoyedic (*op "1", *ketä "2", *näkur "3", *tettə "4", *səmpəleŋkə "5", *məktut "6", *sejtwə "7", *wiət "10")</li>
<li>Finno-Ugric (*üki/*ükti "1", *kormi "3", *ńeljä "4", *wiiti "5", *kuuti "6", *luki "10")
<ul><li>Mansic
<ul><li>Mansi</li>
<li>Hungarian (<i>hét</i> "7"; replacement <i>egy</i> "1")</li></ul></li>
<li>Finno-Khantic (reshaping *kolmi "3" on the analogy of "4")
<ul><li>Khanty</li>
<li>Finno-Permic (reshaping *kektä &gt; *kakta)
<ul><li>Permic</li>
<li>Finno-Volgaic (*śećem "7")
<ul><li>Mari</li>
<li>Finno-Saamic (*kakteksa, *ükteksa "8, 9")
<ul><li>Saamic</li>
<li>Finno-Mordvinic (replacement *kümmen "10" (*luki- "to count", "to read out"))
<ul><li>Mordvinic</li>
<li>Finnic</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonological_isoglosses">Phonological isoglosses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonological isoglosses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another proposed tree, more divergent from the standard, focusing on consonant isoglosses (which does not consider the position of the Samoyedic languages) is presented by Viitso (1997),<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and refined in Viitso (2000):<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Finno-Ugric
<ul><li>Saamic–Fennic (<a href="/wiki/Consonant_gradation" title="Consonant gradation">consonant gradation</a>)
<ul><li>Saamic</li>
<li>Fennic</li></ul></li>
<li>Eastern Finno-Ugric
<ul><li>Mordva</li>
<li>(node)
<ul><li>Mari</li>
<li>Permian–Ugric (*δ &gt; *l)
<ul><li>Permian</li>
<li>Ugric (*s *š *ś &gt; *ɬ *ɬ *s)
<ul><li>Hungarian</li>
<li>Khanty</li>
<li>Mansi</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>The grouping of the four bottom-level branches remains to some degree open to interpretation, with competing models of Finno-Saamic vs. Eastern Finno-Ugric (Mari, Mordvinic, Permic-Ugric; *k &gt; ɣ between vowels, degemination of stops) and Finno-Volgaic (Finno-Saamic, Mari, Mordvinic; *δʲ &gt; *ð between vowels) vs. Permic-Ugric. Viitso finds no evidence for a Finno-Permic grouping.
</p><p>Extending this approach to cover the Samoyedic languages suggests affinity with Ugric, resulting in the aforementioned East Uralic grouping, as it also shares the same sibilant developments. A further non-trivial Ugric-Samoyedic isogloss is the reduction *k, *x, *w &gt; ɣ when before *i, and after a vowel (cf. *k &gt; ɣ above), or adjacent to *t, *s, *š, or *ś.<sup id="cite_ref-EastUralic_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EastUralic-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Finno-Ugric consonant developments after Viitso (2000); Samoyedic changes after Sammallahti (1988)<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li>Note: Proto-Khanty *ɬ in many of the dialects yields *t; Häkkinen assumes this also happened in Mansi and Samoyedic.</li></ul>
<p>The inverse relationship between consonant gradation and medial lenition of stops (the pattern also continuing within the three families where gradation <i>is</i> found) is noted by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Helimski" title="Eugene Helimski">Helimski</a> (1995): an original allophonic gradation system between voiceless and voiced stops would have been easily disrupted by a spreading of voicing to previously unvoiced stops as well.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Honkola.2C_et_al._.282013.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Honkola,_et_al._(2013)">Honkola, et al. (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Honkola, et al. (2013)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Computational_phylogenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Computational phylogenetic">computational phylogenetic</a> study by Honkola, et al. (2013)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> classifies the Uralic languages as follows. <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">Estimated divergence dates</a> from Honkola, et al. (2013) are also given.
</p>
<dl><dt>Uralic (5300 <a href="/wiki/YBP" class="mw-redirect" title="YBP">YBP</a>)</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Ugric_languages" title="Finno-Ugric languages">Finno-Ugric</a> (3900 <a href="/wiki/YBP" class="mw-redirect" title="YBP">YBP</a>)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ugric_languages" title="Ugric languages">Ugric</a> (3300 YBP)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ob-Ugric_languages" title="Ob-Ugric languages">Ob-Ugric</a> (1900 YBP)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Khanty_language" title="Khanty language">Khanty</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mansi_language" title="Mansi language">Mansi</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Permic_languages" title="Finno-Permic languages">Finno-Permic</a> (3700 YBP)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Permic_languages" title="Permic languages">Permian</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">Udmurt</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Volgaic_languages" title="Finno-Volgaic languages">Finno-Volgaic</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a> (3200 YBP)</li>
<li>(core branch)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Erzya_language" title="Erzya language">Erzya</a> (2900 YBP) (<a href="/wiki/Mordvinic_languages" title="Mordvinic languages">Mordvinic</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finno-Lappic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Finno-Lappic languages">Finno-Saami</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sami languages">Sami</a> (800 YBP)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a> (1200 YBP)</li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Typology">Typology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Typology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Structural characteristics generally said to be typical of Uralic languages include:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>extensive use of independent <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">agglutination</a>)</li>
<li>a large set of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">grammatical cases</a> marked with agglutinative suffixes (13–14 cases on average; mainly later developments: Proto-Uralic is reconstructed with 6 cases), e.g.:
<ul><li>Erzya: 12 cases</li>
<li>Estonian: 14 cases (15 cases with instructive)</li>
<li>Finnish: 15 cases</li>
<li>Hungarian: 18 cases (together 34 grammatical cases and case-like suffixes)</li>
<li>Inari Sami: 9 cases</li>
<li>Komi: in certain dialects as many as 27 cases</li>
<li>Moksha: 13 cases</li>
<li>Nenets: 7 cases</li>
<li>North Sami: 6 cases</li>
<li>Udmurt: 16 cases</li>
<li>Veps: 24 cases</li></ul></li>
<li>unique Uralic case system, from which all modern Uralic languages derive their case systems.
<ul><li>nominative singular has no case suffix.</li>
<li>accusative and genitive suffixes are nasal sounds (<i>-n</i>, <i>-m</i>, etc.)</li>
<li>three-way distinction in the local case system, with each set of local cases being divided into forms corresponding roughly to "from", "to", and "in/at"; especially evident, e.g. in Hungarian, Finnish and Estonian, which have several sets of local cases, such as the "inner", "outer" and "on top" systems in Hungarian, while in Finnish the "on top" forms have merged to the "outer" forms.</li>
<li>the Uralic locative suffix exists in all Uralic languages in various cases, e.g. Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Superessive_case" title="Superessive case">superessive</a>, Finnish <a href="/wiki/Essive_case" title="Essive case">essive</a> (<i>-na</i>), North Sami <a href="/wiki/Essive_case" title="Essive case">essive</a>, Erzyan <a href="/wiki/Inessive_case" title="Inessive case">inessive</a>, and Nenets <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a>.</li>
<li>the Uralic <a href="/wiki/Lative_case" title="Lative case">lative</a> suffix exists in various cases in many Uralic languages, e.g. Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Illative_case" title="Illative case">illative</a>, Finnish <a href="/wiki/Lative_case" title="Lative case">lative</a> (<i>-s</i> as in <i>ulos</i> 'out' and <i>rannemmas</i> 'more towards the shore'), Erzyan <a href="/wiki/Illative_case" title="Illative case">illative</a>, Komi <a href="/w/index.php?title=Approximative_case&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Approximative case (page does not exist)">approximative</a>, and Northern Sami <a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>a lack of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical gender</a>, including one pronoun for both <i>he</i> and <i>she</i>; for example, <i>hän</i> in Finnish, <i>tämä</i> in Votic, <i>tema</i> in Estonian, <i>sijə</i> in Komi, <i>ő</i> in Hungarian.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Negative_verb" title="Negative verb">negative verb</a>, which exists in almost all Uralic languages (notably absent in Hungarian)</li>
<li>use of postpositions as opposed to prepositions (prepositions are uncommon).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Possessive_suffix" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive suffix">possessive suffixes</a>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> is also used to express possession in some languages, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> <i>mu koer</i>, <a href="/wiki/Spoken_Finnish" class="mw-redirect" title="Spoken Finnish">colloquial Finnish</a> <i>mun koira</i>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sami_language" title="Northern Sami language">Northern Sami</a> <i>mu beana</i> 'my dog' (literally 'dog of me'). Separate <a href="/wiki/Possessive_adjective" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive adjective">possessive adjectives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive pronoun">possessive pronouns</a>, such as <i>my</i> and <i>your</i>, are rare.</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a>, in the Samoyedic, Ob-Ugric and Samic languages and reconstructed for Proto-Uralic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> markers -j (i) and -t (-d, -q) have a common origin (e.g. in Finnish, Estonian, Võro, Erzya, Samic languages, Samoyedic languages). Hungarian, however, has -i- before the possessive suffixes and -k elsewhere. The plural marker -k is also used in the Samic languages, but there is a regular merging of final -k and -t in Samic, so it can come from either ending.</li>
<li>Possessions are expressed by a possessor in the adessive or dative case, the verb "be" (the <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a>, instead of the verb "have") and the possessed with or without a possessive suffix. The grammatical subject of the sentence is thus the possessed. In Finnish, for example, the possessor is in the <a href="/wiki/Adessive_case" title="Adessive case">adessive case</a>: "Minulla on kala", literally "At me is fish", i.e. "I have a fish", whereas in Hungarian, the possessor is in the <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative case</a>, but appears overtly only if it is contrastive, while the possessed has a possessive ending indicating the number and person of the possessor: "(Nekem) van egy halam", literally "(To me [dative]) is a fish-my" ("(For me) there is a fish of mine"), i.e. "(As for me,) I have a fish".</li>
<li>expressions that include a <a href="/wiki/Numeral_(linguistics)" title="Numeral (linguistics)">numeral</a> are singular if they refer to things which form a single group, e.g. "négy csomó" in Hungarian, "njeallje čuolmma" in Northern Sami, "neli sõlme" in Estonian, and "neljä solmua" in Finnish, each of which means "four knots", but the literal approximation is "four knot". (This approximation is accurate only for Hungarian among these examples, as in Northern Sami the noun is in the singular <a href="/wiki/Accusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Accusative">accusative</a>/<a href="/wiki/Genitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Genitive">genitive</a> case and in Finnish and Estonian the singular noun is in the <a href="/wiki/Partitive" title="Partitive">partitive</a> case, such that the number points to a part of a larger mass, like "four of knot(s)".)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">Vowel harmony</a>: this is present in many but by no means all Uralic languages. It exists in Hungarian and various <a href="/wiki/Baltic-Finnic" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic-Finnic">Baltic-Finnic</a> languages, and is present to some degree elsewhere, such as in Mordvinic, Mari, Eastern Khanty, and Samoyedic. It is lacking in Sami, Permic and standard Estonian, while it does exist in <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B5ro_language" title="Võro language">Võro</a> and elsewhere in <a href="/wiki/South_Estonian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="South Estonian language">South Estonian</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Kihnu" title="Kihnu">Kihnu Island</a> subdialect of North Estonian.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> (Although umlaut letters are used in writing Uralic languages, the languages do not exhibit <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">Germanic umlaut</a>; front and back values are intrinsic features of words and modify suffixes, not vice versa as in umlaut.)</li>
<li>Large vowel inventories. For example, some <a href="/wiki/Selkup_language" title="Selkup language">Selkup</a> varieties have over twenty different <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthongs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> has over twenty different <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">Palatalization</a> of consonants; in this context, palatalization means a secondary articulation, where the middle of the tongue is tense. For example, pairs like <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ɲ]</span> – [n], or [c] – [t] are contrasted in Hungarian, as in <i>hattyú</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[hɒcːuː]</span> "swan". Some Sami languages, for example <a href="/wiki/Skolt_Sami_language" title="Skolt Sami language">Skolt Sami</a>, distinguish three degrees: plain &#x27e8;l&#x27e9; [l], palatalized &#x27e8;'l&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[lʲ]</span>, and palatal &#x27e8;lj&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʎ]</span>, where &#x27e8;'l&#x27e9; has a primary alveolar articulation, while &#x27e8;lj&#x27e9; has a primary palatal articulation. Original Uralic palatalization is phonemic, independent of the following vowel and traceable to the millennia-old <a href="/wiki/Proto-Uralic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Uralic">Proto-Uralic</a>. It is different from Slavic palatalization, which is of more recent origin. The <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic languages</a> have lost palatalization, but the eastern varieties have reacquired it, so Finnic palatalization (where extant) was originally dependent on the following vowel and does not correlate to palatalization elsewhere in Uralic.</li>
<li>Lack of phonologically contrastive <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>.</li>
<li>In many Uralic languages, the stress is always on the first syllable, though Nganasan shows (essentially) penultimate stress, and a number of languages of the central region (Erzya, Mari, Udmurt and Komi-Permyak) synchronically exhibit a lexical accent. The Erzya language can vary its stress in words to give specific nuances to sentential meaning.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexicography">Lexicography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lexicography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Basic vocabulary of about 200 words, including body parts (e.g. eye, heart, head, foot, mouth), family members (e.g. father, mother-in-law), animals (e.g. viper, partridge, fish), nature objects (e.g. tree, stone, nest, water), basic verbs (e.g. live, fall, run, make, see, suck, go, die, swim, know), basic pronouns (e.g. who, what, we, you, I), numerals (e.g. two, five); derivatives increase the number of common words.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_cognates">Selected cognates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Selected cognates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The following is a very brief selection of cognates in basic vocabulary across the Uralic family, which may serve to give an idea of the sound changes involved. This is not a list of translations: cognates have a common origin, but their meaning may be shifted and loanwords may have replaced them.
</p>

<p>Orthographical notes: The hacek denotes postalveolar articulation (&#x27e8;ž&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʒ]</span>, &#x27e8;š&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ʃ]</span>, &#x27e8;č&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[t͡ʃ]</span>) (In Northern Sami, (&#x27e8;ž&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[dʒ]</span>), while the acute denotes a secondary palatal articulation (&#x27e8;ś&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[sʲ ~ ɕ]</span>, &#x27e8;ć&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[tsʲ ~ tɕ]</span>, &#x27e8;l&#x27e9; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[lʲ]</span>) or, in Hungarian, vowel length. The Finnish letter &#x27e8;y&#x27e9; and the letter &#x27e8;ü&#x27e9; in other languages represent the high rounded vowel <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[y]</span>; the letters &#x27e8;ä&#x27e9; and &#x27e8;ö&#x27e9; are the front vowels <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[æ]</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[ø]</span>.
</p><p>As is apparent from the list, Finnish is the most conservative of the Uralic languages presented here, with nearly half the words on the list below identical to their Proto-Uralic reconstructions and most of the remainder only having minor changes, such as the conflation of *ś into /s/, or widespread changes such as the loss of *x and alteration of *ï. Finnish has even preserved old Indo-European borrowings relatively unchanged as well. (An example  is <i>porsas</i> ("pig"), loaned from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> <i>*porḱos</i> or pre-<a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> <i>*porśos</i>, unchanged since loaning save for loss of <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalization</a>, *ś &gt; s.)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_intelligibility">Mutual intelligibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mutual intelligibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Estonian philologist <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mall_Hellam&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mall Hellam (page does not exist)">Mall Hellam</a> proposed cognate sentences that she asserted to be mutually intelligible among the three most widely spoken Uralic languages: Finnish, Estonian, and Hungarian:<sup id="cite_ref-Economist_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>: <i lang="et">Elav kala ujub vee all</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>: <i lang="fi">Elävä kala ui veden alla</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>: <i lang="hu">Eleven hal úszik a víz alatt</i>.</li>
<li>English: A living fish swims underwater.</li></ul>
<p>However, linguist <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Pullum" class="mw-redirect" title="Geoffrey Pullum">Geoffrey Pullum</a> reports that neither Finns nor Hungarians could understand the other language's version of the sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison">Comparison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Comparison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li>In <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a>, but not in <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">Udmurt</a>.</li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_relations_with_other_language_families">Proposed relations with other language families</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Proposed relations with other language families">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many relationships between Uralic and other language families have been suggested, but none of these are generally accepted by linguists at the present time: All of the following hypotheses are minority views at the present time in Uralic studies.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uralic-Yukaghir">Uralic-Yukaghir</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Uralic-Yukaghir">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uralic%E2%80%93Yukaghir_languages" title="Uralic–Yukaghir languages">Uralic–Yukaghir languages</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Uralic%E2%80%93Yukaghir_languages" title="Uralic–Yukaghir languages">Uralic–Yukaghir</a> hypothesis identifies Uralic and <a href="/wiki/Yukaghir_languages" title="Yukaghir languages">Yukaghir</a> as independent members of a single language 
family. It is currently widely accepted that the similarities between Uralic and Yukaghir languages are due to ancient contacts.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Regardless, the hypothesis is accepted by a few linguists and viewed as attractive by a somewhat larger number.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eskimo-Uralic">Eskimo-Uralic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Eskimo-Uralic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Uralic_languages" title="Eskimo–Uralic languages">Eskimo–Uralic languages</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Uralic_languages" title="Eskimo–Uralic languages">Eskimo–Uralic</a> hypothesis associates Uralic with the <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" title="Eskimo–Aleut languages">Eskimo–Aleut languages</a>. This is an old thesis whose antecedents go back to the 18th century. An important restatement of it is <a href="/wiki/Knut_Bergsland" title="Knut Bergsland">Bergsland</a> 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uralo-Siberian">Uralo-Siberian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Uralo-Siberian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uralo-Siberian_languages" title="Uralo-Siberian languages">Uralo-Siberian languages</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Uralo-Siberian_languages" title="Uralo-Siberian languages">Uralo-Siberian</a> is an expanded form of the Eskimo–Uralic hypothesis. It associates Uralic with Yukaghir, <a href="/wiki/Chukotko-Kamchatkan_languages" title="Chukotko-Kamchatkan languages">Chukotko-Kamchatkan</a>, and Eskimo–Aleut. It was propounded by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fortescue" title="Michael Fortescue">Michael Fortescue</a> in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> It is currently the most supported hypothesis regarding close relatives of Uralic. Modern supporters include <a href="/wiki/Morris_Swadesh" title="Morris Swadesh">Morris Swadesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juha_Janhunen" title="Juha Janhunen">Juha Janhunen</a> and Häkkinen. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fortescue" title="Michael Fortescue">Michael Fortescue</a> (2017) present next to new linguistic evidence also several genetic studies, that support a common origin of the included groups, with a suggested homeland somewhere in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Asia" title="Northeast Asia">Northeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ural-Altaic">Ural-Altaic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ural-Altaic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ural%E2%80%93Altaic_languages" title="Ural–Altaic languages">Ural–Altaic languages</a></div>
<p>Theories proposing a close relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic languages</a> were formerly popular, based on similarities in vocabulary as well as in grammatical and phonological features, in particular the similarities in the Uralic and Altaic pronouns and the presence of <a href="/wiki/Agglutination" title="Agglutination">agglutination</a> in both sets of languages, as well as <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> in some. For example, the word for "language" is similar in <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a> (<i>keel</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a> (<i>хэл</i> (<i>hel</i>)). These theories are now generally rejected<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and most such similarities are attributed to <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a> or coincidence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indo-Uralic">Indo-Uralic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Indo-Uralic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Uralic_languages" title="Indo-Uralic languages">Indo-Uralic languages</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Uralic_languages" title="Indo-Uralic languages">Indo-Uralic</a> (or Uralo-Indo-European) hypothesis suggests that Uralic and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> are related at a fairly close level or, in its stronger form, that they are more closely related than either is to any other language family.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uralo-Dravidian">Uralo-Dravidian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Uralo-Dravidian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The hypothesis that the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian languages</a> display similarities with the Uralic language group, suggesting a prolonged period of contact in the past,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> is popular amongst Dravidian linguists and has been supported by a number of scholars, including <a href="/wiki/Robert_Caldwell" title="Robert Caldwell">Robert Caldwell</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burrow" title="Thomas Burrow">Thomas Burrow</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kamil_Zvelebil" title="Kamil Zvelebil">Kamil Zvelebil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and Mikhail Andronov.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> This hypothesis has, however, been rejected by some specialists in Uralic languages,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and has in recent times also been criticised by other Dravidian linguists, such as <a href="/wiki/Bhadriraju_Krishnamurti" title="Bhadriraju Krishnamurti">Bhadriraju Krishnamurti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nostratic">Nostratic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nostratic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nostratic_languages" title="Nostratic languages">Nostratic languages</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nostratic_languages" title="Nostratic languages">Nostratic</a> associates Uralic, Indo-European, Altaic, Dravidian, and various other language families of Asia. The Nostratic hypothesis was first propounded by <a href="/wiki/Holger_Pedersen_(linguist)" title="Holger Pedersen (linguist)">Holger Pedersen</a> in 1903<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently revived by <a href="/wiki/Vladislav_Illich-Svitych" title="Vladislav Illich-Svitych">Vladislav Illich-Svitych</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aharon_Dolgopolsky" title="Aharon Dolgopolsky">Aharon Dolgopolsky</a> in the 1960s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eurasiatic">Eurasiatic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Eurasiatic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eurasiatic_languages" title="Eurasiatic languages">Eurasiatic languages</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eurasiatic_languages" title="Eurasiatic languages">Eurasiatic</a> resembles Nostratic in including Uralic, Indo-European, and Altaic, but differs from it in excluding the South Caucasian languages, Dravidian, and Afroasiatic and including Chukotko-Kamchatkan, <a href="/wiki/Nivkh_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Nivkh language">Nivkh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ainu languages">Ainu</a>, and Eskimo–Aleut. It was propounded by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Greenberg" title="Joseph Greenberg">Joseph Greenberg</a> in 2000–2002.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Similar ideas had earlier been expressed by Heinrich Koppelmann in 1933 and by <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B6rn_Collinder" title="Björn Collinder">Björn Collinder</a> in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uralic_skepticism">Uralic skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Uralic skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In her book, <i>The Uralic language family: facts, myths, and statistics</i>, linguist Angela Marcantonio argues against the existence of the Uralic family, claiming that the languages are no more closely related to each other than they are to various other Eurasian languages. According to her the several subgroups of Uralic do not form a genetic unity. She continues to argue that Hungarian is not demonstrably related to the Ob-Ugric languages (or other included branches) and thus a language isolate.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Marcantonio's proposal has been strongly dismissed by most reviewers as unfounded and methodolocially flawed.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> A more moderate review was made by linguist <a href="/wiki/Edward_J._Vajda" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward J. Vajda">Edward J. Vajda</a>. Although he also rejects the book's main proposal (i.e. the author's dismissal of Uralic as a language family), he partially agrees with Marcantonio's critique and argues that both Uralicists and non-Uralicists should take the critique by Marcantonio seriously.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_comparisons">Other comparisons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uralic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other comparisons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various unorthodox comparisons have been advanced. These are considered at best spurious fringe-theories by specialists:
</p>
<ul><li>Finno-<a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a><sup id="cite_ref-Trask_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trask-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li>Hungarian-Etruscan<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cal-Ugrian_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Cal-Ugrian theory">Cal-Ugrian theory</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Obsolete_theories_of_the_Hungarian_language_relations" title="Obsolete theories of the Hungarian language relations">Obsolete theories of the Hungarian language relations</a></li></ul>
